Is Continuation an Additional Concept?
Razi presents two arguments on whether continuation is an additional concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments regarding whether the continuation of the divine essence is an additional attribute, and the logical difficulties each raises.

Core Idea
The debate over continuation reveals deep logical issues about attributes and regress.

In this first investigation, Imam Razi examines whether the continuation (istimrar) of Allah's essence is an additional concept beyond the essence itself. This question is foundational for understanding divine attributes. Razi presents two opposing positions, each with its own reasoning and critique. By analyzing these arguments, we see the complexity of speaking about God's permanence without falling into logical contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: Razi introduces the question, states the first view, then an objection, then the second view.
Focus on how each argument is built and what its consequences are.
2
Identify the two views and the objection
The first view claims continuation is additional. The objection shows it leads to infinite regress. The second view says it is not additional, using the example of non-existence.
Which argument seems stronger to you at first glance?
3
Reflect on the logical puzzles
Consider: If continuation were additional, why would it lead to regress? If not additional, how can non-existence have continuity? These puzzles show the depth of the topic.
Razi does not settle the debate here, but raises crucial issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن في هذه المسألة أبحاثا ثلاثة : البحث الأول: أن استمرار الذات هل هو مفهوم زائد على الذات أم لا؟ فقال قوم: إنه مفهوم زائد على الذات ؛ وذلك لأن الذات كانت حاصلة الزمان الأول، ولم يكن استمرار الذات حاصلا في الزمان الأول، فلما حصل في الزمان الثاني حصل الذات وحصل استمرار الذات وهذا يقتضي أن يكون استمرار الذات مغايرا لنفس الذات. وعلى هذا الكلام إشكال وهو أنه كلما صدق على الذات أنها صارت مستمرة بعد أن لم تكن كذلك صدق عليها أنها كانت متجددة في الزمان الأول ثم في الزمان الثاني صارت غير متجددة، فقد صارت غير متجددة بعد أن كانت موصوفة بأنها متجددة فيلزم أن يكون حدوثها وتجددها صفة زائدة على ذاتها، وهذا محال ؛ لأنه لو كان الحدوث صفة زائدة لكانت تلك الصفة حادثة، ويلزم التسلسل. وقال آخرون: استمرار الذات ليس صفة زائدة على الذات وذلك لأن العدم يوصف بالاستمرار ، فلو كان الاستمرار وصفًا ثابتا لزم اتصاف النفي بالصفة الثابتة، وذلك محال.
And know that concerning this question there are three investigations: The first investigation: whether the continuation (istimrar) of the essence is an additional concept (mafhum za'id) to the essence or not. Some people said: It is an additional concept to the essence, and that is because the essence existed in the first moment, and the continuation of the essence did not exist in the first moment. When it came to be in the second moment, the essence came to be and the continuation of the essence came to be, and this requires that the continuation of the essence be other than the essence itself. However, there is an objection to this statement: whenever it is true of the essence that it became continuous after not being so, it is true of it that it was temporally originated in the first moment and then in the second moment became non-originated. Thus it became non-originated after being described as originated, which would require that its origination (huduth) and temporality (tajaddud) be an attribute additional to its essence, and this is impossible because if huduth were an additional attribute, that attribute would be originated, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow. Others said: The continuation of the essence is not an additional attribute to the essence, and that is because non-existence (al-'adam) is described as having continuation. If continuation were a fixed attribute, it would entail that negation (al-nafy) be described by a fixed attribute, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is the first of three investigations concerning baqa. It addresses whether 'continuation' (istimrar) of the essence is an additional concept. Razi presents two opposing views. The first view argues that since the essence existed at time one without continuation, and at time two with continuation, continuation must be distinct from the essence. However, an objection is raised: if we say the essence became continuous, that implies it was once 'originated' and then became 'non-originated', which would require origination (huduth) to be an added attribute—but that leads to infinite regress. The second view argues that continuation cannot be a real attribute because even non-existence is said to 'continue' (e.g., 'the absence of a thing continues'), so if continuation were a fixed attribute, it would attach to a negation, which is absurd. Thus both arguments aim to show difficulties with the concept of continuation as an added attribute.

Key Terms
استمرار
مفهوم زائد
التسلسل
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the objection to the first argument proves the second argument.
Correction: Razi does not endorse either view; he presents both with their own difficulties. The objection shows the first view's flaw, but the second view has its own problematic implication about non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against the first group's argument that continuation is an additional concept?
A) It leads to an infinite regress in attributing huduth
B) It implies that non-existence can have a fixed attribute
C) It contradicts the definition of baqa
D) It relies on a false premise about time

Key Takeaway
The question of whether continuation (istimrar) is an additional attribute is logically problematic, with both views facing serious objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that both the claim that continuation is an additional concept and its negation lead to absurdities. Are you willing to accept that your finite mind cannot fully capture divine attributes? What does this humility cost you in your daily reliance on rational arguments about God?
?
If non-existence can be described as 'continuous', what does that imply about the subtlety of the concept 'continuation'? How might this awareness affect the way you speak about God's attributes in conversation with others or in your own heart?

Razi's First Two Proofs Against Baqa
Razi enumerates his first two proofs against baqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first two arguments against the need for a separate cause of continued existence (baqa).

Core Idea
The ruling of a single thing cannot change from need to independence.

Razi begins the second investigation by stating that the correct view is that a substance does not need a separate cause (called baqa) for its continued existence. He then presents two proofs: one based on the constancy of a thing's ruling, and another on the absurdity of causing an already existent thing to exist. Read the passage to see how he argues.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the two proofs Razi presents.
Notice the logical structure: each proof starts with 'الحجة الأولى/الثانية'.
2
Identify the first proof
Find the first proof. It argues that the substance's existence in the second moment is distinct from the first, but since it was independent in the first, it must remain independent.
The key principle is that the ruling (حكم) of a single thing cannot change.
3
Identify the second proof
Find the second proof. It argues that if baqa caused existence, it would be causing something already existing, which is impossible.
This argument targets the idea of baqa being an efficient cause.

البحث الثاني: هو أن الجوهر في الزمان الثاني هل يحتاج على معنى يقتضي وجوده في الزمان الثاني أم لا ؟ فمن الناس من أثبته وسماه بالبقاء والحق أنه محال، ويدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى: أن وجود الجوهر في الزمان الثاني، غير وجوده في الزمان الأول، لكن وجوده في الزمان الأول كان غنيا عن هذا العرض، فوجوده في الزمان الثاني يجب أن يكون غنيا عنه ؛ لأن حكم الشيء الواحد يمتنع أن يتبدل بالحاجة والاستغناء. الحجة الثانية : وجود الجوهر في الزمان الثاني لو كان معللا بهذا العرض لكان هذا العرض مؤثرًا في إيجاد الموجود، وهو محال. الحجة الثالثة : لما كان العرض محتاجًا في تحققه وثبوته إلى جوهر امتنع كون الجوهر محتاجًا في وجوده إلى شيء من الأعراض، وإلا وقع الدور. الحجة الرابعة : شرط قيام البقاء بالجوهر حصول الجوهر في الزمان الثاني فلو كان حصول الجوهر في الزمان الثاني معللا بالبقاء لزم الدور، وهو محال. واحتج مثبتو البقاء بهذا التفسير فقالوا : الجوهر حصل في الزمان الثاني مع جواز أن يصير معدوماً، والجائز مفتقر إلى المخصص، ويستحيل أن يكون ذلك المخصص إلا البقاء فكان القول بوجود البقاء حقاً وهذا الكلام مبني على مقدمات : المقدمة الأولى : أن الجوهر حصل في الزمان الثاني، والخلاف فيه مع النظام - كما يقال - فإنه زعم أن الجسم متجدد حالا بعد حال والدليل على كون الجوهر جائز البقاء لذاته أن كون ماهيته قابلة للوجود أمر من لوازم الذات، فتكون هذه القابلية حاصلة أبدا، وإذا كان كذلك كان جائز الوجود في الزمان الثاني والثالث وذلك يقتضي كون الجسم جائز البقاء، واعلم أن هذا الكلام متين إلا أنه يلزم عليه صحة بقاء الأعراض. المقدمة الثانية: أن الجوهر في الزمان الثاني جائز العدم، وزعم قوم أنه بعد
The second investigation: whether the substance (al-jawhar) in the second moment needs a meaning (ma'na) that necessitates its existence in the second moment or not. Some people affirmed this and called it baqa, but the truth is that it is impossible, and there are proofs for that: The first proof: the existence of the substance in the second moment is other than its existence in the first moment, but its existence in the first moment was independent of this accident ('arad), so its existence in the second moment must be independent of it, because the ruling of a single thing cannot change between need and independence. The second proof: if the existence of the substance in the second moment were caused by this accident, then this accident would be effective in bringing the existent into existence, which is impossible. The third proof: since the accident needs a substance for its realization and establishment, it is impossible that the substance needs any of the accidents for its existence, otherwise circularity (dawr) would occur. The fourth proof: the condition for baqa's inherence in the substance is the obtaining of the substance in the second moment; if the obtaining of the substance in the second moment were caused by baqa, circularity would follow, which is impossible. The affirmers of baqa argued by this interpretation, saying: The substance came to be in the second moment together with the possibility of becoming non-existent, and what is possible needs a particularizer (mukhassis), and it is impossible that this particularizer be other than baqa, so the statement of the existence of baqa is true. This statement is based on premises: The first premise: that the substance came to be in the second moment, and the dispute with al-Nazzam about it – as it is said – for he held that the body is renewed moment after moment. The proof that the substance is possible of persisting (ja'iz al-baqa) by itself is that its quiddity (mahiyya) being receptive to existence is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the essence, so this receptivity exists always, and if so, it is possible of existence in the second and third moments, and that entails that the body is possible of persisting. Know that this statement is sound, except that it entails the validity of the persistence of accidents (baqa al-a'rad). The second premise: that the substance in the second moment is possible of non-existence, and some people claimed that after...
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is the second investigation, addressing whether a substance (jawhar) needs a separate cause (called baqa) to continue existing in the second moment. Razi states that the correct view is that such a cause is impossible, and he gives four arguments: (1) The substance's existence in the second moment is distinct from its existence in the first, but it was independent of any accident in the first moment, so it should remain independent; (2) If baqa caused existence, it would be creating an already existing thing, which is absurd; (3) Accidents depend on substances, so substances cannot depend on accidents (circularity would occur); (4) The inherence of baqa requires the substance to already exist in the second moment, so if existence were caused by baqa, that would be circular. Then Razi presents the counter-argument of the affirmers of baqa: The substance exists in the second moment while being possibly non-existent, so it needs a particularizer (mukhassis), which must be baqa. This argument is built on two premises. The first premise is that the substance exists in the second moment (contra al-Nazzam who denies persistence), and a proof is given that substance is by nature possible of persistence because its quiddity is receptive to existence always, so it can exist at any time. Razi notes this proof is sound but entails the persistence of accidents, which is problematic. The second premise begins but is cut off: the substance is possible of non-existence, and some claimed... The text ends here.

Key Terms
الجوهر
العرض
البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that continued existence (baqa) is a necessary attribute for a substance to persist.
Correction: Razi argues that the substance's existence in the second moment is a new existence, and there is no need for a separate cause. The substance was independent in the first moment, so it must remain independent.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first argument, why should the substance be independent of baqa in the second moment?
A) Because baqa does not exist in the second moment
B) Because the substance was independent in the first moment and the ruling of a single thing cannot change
C) Because the substance exists necessarily
D) Because the substance is eternal

Key Takeaway
Razi's first two proofs show that the concept of baqa as a cause of continued existence is logically incoherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often rely on created things—health, wealth, relationships—as if they have permanence. How does Razi's first proof, that a thing's ruling cannot change from independence to need, expose the illusion that these things can ever truly sustain you? What will you do differently tomorrow to place your trust only in the Self-Sufficient?
?
Razi argues that if baqa were a cause, it would absurdly try to bring into existence something already existent. Consider a habit or comfort you think 'keeps you going.' In what way is your attachment to it similar to the impossible baqa—expecting it to cause your well-being when you already exist? What step will you take today to break that illusion?
?
This lesson shows that the atom of your existence is renewed moment by moment, independent of any created cause. Do you live as if your next breath depends on Allah's direct creation, or do you feel entitled to it? How will you remind yourself of your constant need for the Creator before you sleep tonight?

Razi's Circularity Arguments Against Baqa
Razi presents two circularity-based proofs against baqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third and fourth proofs, which use circularity (dawr) to refute the need for baqa.

Core Idea
If substance depends on accident, and accident depends on substance, circularity follows.

Razi continues his refutation of baqa by presenting two arguments that lead to circularity. The third proof shows that if a substance needed an accident for its existence, a circular dependency would occur because accidents themselves depend on substances. The fourth proof shows that baqa's inherence requires the substance to already exist, leading to another circularity. Read the passage to see how these arguments work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the logical structure of the third and fourth proofs.
Both proofs rely on the concept of circularity (دور).
2
Understand the third proof
The third proof argues: accidents need substances to exist. If substances needed accidents, there would be circular dependency.
This is based on the metaphysical principle that accidents are accidents of substances.
3
Understand the fourth proof
The fourth proof argues: baqa's inherence in a substance requires the substance to already exist in the second moment. If baqa caused that existence, circularity results.
This is a temporal circularity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What is the common feature of Razi's third and fourth proofs?
A) They both rely on the concept of possibility

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you often find yourself making circular excuses to avoid accountability? For example, saying 'I can’t pray because I’m too busy' while your busyness is caused by neglecting spiritual priorities. How will you break this cycle starting today?
?
Razi shows that if something depends on another, it cannot be the cause of that other. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes treat His blessings as if they are causes for your obedience, rather than the other way around? What would change if you truly recognized that your obedience depends entirely on His grace?
?
When you reflect on your own existence and attributes, do you ever assume that your good qualities are self-sustained? How does realizing that every quality (including baqa) depends on its substrate humble you to rely only on Allah, the Self-Subsistent?

The Defenders' Argument for Baqa and Its Premises
Razi presents the defenders' baqa argument with two premises, the second incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the defenders' argument for baqa, which is based on the need for a particularizer (mukhassis), and its two premises, noting that the second premise is incomplete in the text.

Core Idea
The defenders argue that since the substance is possibly non-existent, it needs a particularizer, which they claim is baqa.

After presenting his refutations, Razi now presents the argument of those who affirm baqa. They argue that the substance exists in the second moment as a possibility for non-existence, so it needs a particularizer (mukhassis) to keep it in existence, and that particularizer is baqa. This argument rests on two premises; the first premise is defended with a proof, but the text cuts off during the second premise. Read the passage to understand the structure and the first premise's proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that this is the defenders' argument; Razi is presenting it before critiquing it.
The text ends abruptly during the second premise.
2
Identify the main argument
The defenders say: the substance exists in the second moment yet is possible of non-existence. What is possible needs a particularizer. That particularizer is baqa.
This is a standard kalam argument for the need for a cause of persistence.
3
Understand the first premise
The first premise is that the substance exists in the second moment (contra al-Nazzam). The proof: its quiddity is receptive to existence always, so it can exist at any time.
Razi comments that this proof is sound but entails the persistence of accidents, which is problematic.
4
Note the incomplete second premise
The second premise states that the substance is possible of non-existence. The text then breaks off. Some claimed something else, but the argument is incomplete.
The OCR may have truncated the text.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The defenders' argument is sound and proves baqa exists.
Correction: Razi shows that the proof for the first premise entails the persistence of accidents (بقاء الأعراض), which is problematic for the defenders. The argument is not fully accepted.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say about the proof for the first premise of the defenders' argument?
A) It is sound but entails the validity of the persistence of accidents
B) It is completely false
C) It proves that baqa is necessary
D) It is based on a misinterpretation of quiddity

Key Takeaway
The defenders argue that baqa is needed as a particularizer for a possible substance, but Razi notes that the proof for the first premise leads to an unwanted conclusion about the persistence of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence at this very moment depends on a particularizer (mukhassis) to keep you from nothingness, do you truly feel that dependency in your daily life, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you lived each moment conscious that you are 'possible of non-existence'?
?
Razi notes that the proof for persistence of the substance leads to an unwanted conclusion: the persistence of accidents. Consider your own attributes—your knowledge, your moods, your strength. Do you assume they persist by themselves? How does the fragility of your transient states make you more aware that only Allah's attribute of baqa is truly self-subsisting?

Four Proofs Against the Necessity of Baqa'
Razi enumerates four proofs against baqa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four logical proofs from Imam al-Razi establishing that a substance does not require an accident called baqa' (permanence) to continue existing in the second moment of time.

Core Idea
A substance needs no accident to persist; baqa' leads to circularity and impossibility.

Imam al-Razi tackles a key theological question: does a substance need a special attribute (baqa') to keep existing after the first moment? Some theologians affirmed it, but Razi shows it is impossible. In this passage, he presents four compelling proofs that a substance is self-sufficient in its continuance. These arguments are essential for understanding the Ash'ari rejection of accidental permanence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage without analysis. Then read the full English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: Razi states the issue, declares the truth, then provides four numbered proofs.
2
Analyze each proof individually
Go through each proof one by one. Identify the premise, the reasoning, and the conclusion for each. The commentary will help clarify.
Proof 1 uses the principle of consistency; Proof 2 addresses causation; Proof 3 avoids circularity; Proof 4 highlights conditional dependence.
3
Reflect on the interconnectedness
Consider how the proofs work together. They are not independent; each attacks a different aspect of the claim that baqa' is needed.
The common misunderstanding is that they are separate; they complement each other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : هو أن الجوهر في الزمان الثاني هل يحتاج على معنى يقتضي وجوده في الزمان الثاني أم لا ؟ فمن الناس من أثبته وسماه بالبقاء والحق أنه محال، ويدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى: أن وجود الجوهر في الزمان الثاني، غير وجوده في الزمان الأول، لكن وجوده في الزمان الأول كان غنيا عن هذا العرض، فوجوده في الزمان الثاني يجب أن يكون غنيا عنه؛ لأن حكم الشيء الواحد يمتنع أن يتبدل بالحاجة والاستغناء. الحجة الثانية : وجود الجوهر في الزمان الثاني لو كان معللا بهذا العرض لكان هذا العرض مؤثرًا في إيجاد الموجود، وهو محال. الحجة الثالثة : لما كان العرض محتاجًا في تحققه وثبوته إلى جوهر امتنع كون الجوهر محتاجًا في وجوده إلى شيء من الأعراض، وإلا وقع الدور. الحجة الرابعة : شرط قيام البقاء بالجوهر حصول الجوهر في الزمان الثاني فلو كان حصول الجوهر في الزمان الثاني معللا بالبقاء لزم الدور، وهو محال.
The second investigation: whether the substance in the second moment of time needs a meaning that necessitates its existence in the second moment or not. Some people affirmed it and called it baqa' (permanence), but the truth is that it is impossible. And [the following] proofs indicate that: The first proof: that the existence of the substance in the second moment is other than its existence in the first moment, but its existence in the first moment was independent of this accident, so its existence in the second moment must be independent of it; because the judgment of a single thing cannot change between need and independence. The second proof: if the existence of the substance in the second moment were caused by this accident, then this accident would be effective in bringing into existence that which already exists, which is impossible. The third proof: since the accident in its actualization and establishment needs a substance, it is impossible for the substance to be in need in its existence of any accident, otherwise circularity would occur. The fourth proof: the condition for the subsistence of baqa' in the substance is the occurrence of the substance in the second moment; so if the occurrence of the substance in the second moment were caused by baqa', circularity would necessarily occur, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents four proofs against the claim that a substance needs an accident called baqa' (permanence) to continue existing in the second moment of time. Razi states the issue: does the substance in the second moment need a separate meaning that necessitates its existence? Some affirm it and call it baqa', but he declares it impossible.

Proof 1: The existence of the substance in the second moment is a different existence from that in the first moment. The first existence was independent of any such accident; therefore the second existence must also be independent, because a single entity cannot change from being independent to being needful.

Proof 2: If the substance's existence in the second moment were caused by this accident, then the accident would be an agent bringing into existence something that already exists (since the substance is already present), which is absurd.

Proof 3: Accidents themselves depend on substances for their existence. If a substance needed an accident for its own existence, a circular dependency would result.

Proof 4: For baqa' to inhere in the substance, the substance must already exist in the second moment. If that existence itself were caused by baqa', we would have circularity: the substance's existence is both the condition for and the effect of baqa'. Thus baqa' is impossible.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
بقاء
دور
استغناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four proofs are independent and unrelated.
Correction: They are interconnected and build on each other to refute baqa' from multiple angles.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why must the substance's existence in the second moment be independent of baqa'?
A) Because the substance is eternal and does not need any cause.
B) Because the first existence was independent of baqa', and the same substance cannot change in its need.
C) Because baqa' is a nonexistent accident that cannot affect anything.
D) Because the substance is created anew in each moment.

Key Takeaway
A substance does not need baqa' to continue; the concept leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first proof argues that a substance's existence in the second moment must be independent because its first existence was independent. In what area of your life do you assume you need a 'crutch' to continue, when in reality you already have what you need? Will you trust that strength today?
?
The second proof shows that an accident cannot bring into existence something that already exists. Do you ever act as though your good deeds 'create' your worth rather than reflecting it? How can you shift from earning to acknowledging God's already-given existence?
?
The circularity in proof 4 reveals that baqa' requires the substance to exist first, but if it causes existence, it's a vicious circle. Are there spiritual commitments you maintain by circular reasoning—doing them out of habit without genuine reliance on God? What would it take to break that cycle?

The Three Premises Supporting Baqa'
Razi presents the three premises of the argument for baqa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three premises used to argue for the existence of baqa', including their proofs and Razi's assessment.

Core Idea
Baqa' is argued by necessity: the possible needs a particularizer, and that must be baqa'.

Before delving into the different interpretations of how things persist, Razi presents the foundational argument used by those who affirm baqa' as an attribute. This argument relies on three key premises about the possibility of existence and non-existence. Understanding these premises is essential for grasping the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall argument
Read the opening sentences to see how the affirmers of baqa' structure their claim: the substance is possible of non-existence, needs a particularizer, which must be baqa'.
This is a concise logical argument.
2
Examine each premise
Go through the three premises one by one. Note the proof given for each: the first premise about possibility of permanence, the second about possibility of non-existence, and the third that the possible needs an exigent.
Pay attention to Razi's comment that the first premise is solid but entails permanence of accidents.
3
Reflect on the structure
Consider how the three premises together lead to the conclusion that baqa' exists. Razi presents them as a foundation, and later he will examine the possible exigents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج مثبتو البقاء بهذا التفسير فقالوا : الجوهر حصل في الزمان الثاني مع جواز أن يصير معدوماً، والجائز مفتقر إلى المخصص، ويستحيل أن يكون ذلك المخصص إلا البقاء فكان القول بوجود البقاء حقاً. وهذا الكلام مبني على مقدمات : المقدمة الأولى : أن الجوهر حصل في الزمان الثاني، والخلاف فيه مع النظام -كما يقال- فإنه زعم أن الجسم متجدد حالا بعد حال والدليل على كون الجوهر جائز البقاء لذاته أن كون ماهيته قابلة للوجود أمر من لوازم الذات، فتكون هذه القابلية حاصلة أبدا، وإذا كان كذلك كان جائز الوجود في الزمان الثاني والثالث وذلك يقتضي كون الجسم جائز البقاء، واعلم أن هذا الكلام متين إلا أنه يلزم عليه صحة بقاء الأعراض. المقدمة الثانية : أن الجوهر في الزمان الثاني جائز العدم، وزعم قوم أنه بعد حدوثه يمتنع عليه العدم والدليل على كونه جائز العدم أن ماهيته قبل وجوده كانت موصوفة بالعدم، فثبت أن هذه الماهية قابلة للعدم وهذه القابلية من لوازم هذه الماهية فهذه الماهية قابلة للعدم أبدا، وهذا يقتضي كون الجسم جائز العدم في الزمان الثاني. المقدمة الثالثة لما ثبت أن الجسم حصل في الزمان الثاني مع جواز أن يكون معدوما فالجائز لا بد له من المقتضي، وذلك المقتضي إما أن يكون أمراً عدمياً أو وجودياً، وعلى التقديرين فإما أن يكون مختاراً أو موجباً، فهذه أقسام أربعة : القسم الأول : أن يقال : إنه ربما بقي لأن الفاعل المختار ما أعدمه، وهذا قول من قال : الإعدام بالقدرة جائز - وهو أحد قولي القاضي أبي بكر من أصحابنا وقول أبي الحسن الخياط من قدماء المعتزلة، وهو قول محمود الخوارزمي من متأخري المعتزلة - ومن الناس من أنكر ذلك، وقال: القدرة صفة مؤثرة، والعدم نفي محض، فإسناد العدم إلى القدرة محال. والقسم الثاني: أن يقال : إنه إنما بقي لأن له ضدّاً متى وجد لزم من وجوده عدمه، وإذا لم يوجد ذلك الضد لزم بقاؤه، وهذا مذهب أبي علي وأبي هاشم من المعتزلة وزعموا أن ذلك الضد هو عرض يخلقه الله تعالى لا في محل ويسمونه بالبقاء. والقاضي عبد الجبار بن أحمد وجمهور أصحابنا أبطلوا هذا القول من وجهين : الحجة الأولى : أن المضادة حاصلة من الجانبين فليس انقطاع الباقي لطريان الحادث أولى من اندفاع الحادث لوجود الباقي، وقالوا بل اندفاع الحادث لوجود الباقي أولى، وذلك لأن الحادث أضعف من الباقي بدليل أن الحادث لا يوجد إلا عند المقتضي، والباقي يبقى بدون المقتضي، وذلك يدل على أن الباقي أقوى. الحجة الثانية : أن طريان الضد الحادث مشروط بزوال الضد الباقي، فلو كان زوال الضد الباقي معللاً بطريان الضد الحادث لزم الدور، وهو محال. القسم الثالث: أن يقال : الجوهر إنما بقي لأن الفاعل المختار يبقيه، قالوا : وهذا أيضاً محال وذلك لأن إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل محال. والقسم الرابع : وهو أنه إنما بقي لأنه قام بالجوهر في الزمان الثاني أعراض مقتضية لبقاء ذلك الجوهر، ثم نقول : ذلك العرض إما أن يكون هو من الأعراض المشهورة - وهي الألوان والطعوم والروائح والإرادات والقدر وأمثالها - وإما أن يكون عرضاً زائداً عليها مغايراً لها.
And the affirmers of baqa' argued with this interpretation and said: The substance occurs in the second moment while it is possible for it to become non-existent, and the possible needs a particularizer, and it is impossible for that particularizer to be other than baqa', so the statement of the existence of baqa' is true. And this speech is based on premises: The first premise: that the substance occurs in the second moment—and the disagreement with al-Nazzam about it, as it is said, for he claimed that the body is renewed state after state—and the proof that the substance is possible of permanence in itself is that its quiddity being receptive of existence is a necessary concomitant of the essence, so this receptivity is always present; and if so, it is possible of existence in the second and third moment, and that necessitates that the body is possible of permanence. Know that this speech is solid except that it entails the validity of the permanence of accidents. The second premise: that the substance in the second moment is possible of non-existence; and some people claimed that after its occurrence non-existence becomes impossible for it. The proof of its being possible of non-existence is that its quiddity before its existence was described by non-existence, so it is established that this quiddity is receptive of non-existence, and this receptivity is a necessary concomitant of this quiddity, so this quiddity is always receptive of non-existence, and this necessitates that the body is possible of non-existence in the second moment. The third premise: since it is established that the body occurs in the second moment while it is possible to be non-existent, so the possible must have an exigent, and that exigent is either a non-existential matter or an existential matter, and on both suppositions it is either a free agent or a necessitating agent. So these are four divisions: The first division: that it is said: it perhaps remained because the free agent did not annihilate it, and this is the saying of those who said: annihilation by power is possible—and this is one of the two sayings of al-Qadi Abu Bakr from our companions, and the saying of Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat from the early Mu'tazila, and the saying of Mahmud al-Khwarizmi from the later Mu'tazila—and some people denied that and said: power is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation, so attributing non-existence to power is impossible. The second division: that it is said: it remained because it has an opposite; when it exists, its existence necessitates its non-existence, and if that opposite does not exist, its permanence is necessitated. This is the school of Abu 'Ali and Abu Hashim from the Mu'tazila, and they claimed that that opposite is an accident which Allah creates not in a locus and they call it baqa'. And al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad and the majority of our companions invalidated this statement from two aspects: The first proof: that opposition occurs from both sides, so the cessation of the permanent due to the occurrence of the originated is not more appropriate than the repulsion of the originated due to the existence of the permanent; rather they said the repulsion of the originated due to the existence of the permanent is more appropriate, because the originated is weaker than the permanent as evidenced by the fact that the originated does not exist except upon an exigent, while the permanent remains without an exigent, and that indicates that the permanent is stronger. The second proof: that the occurrence of the originated opposite is conditional upon the removal of the permanent opposite; so if the removal of the permanent opposite were caused by the occurrence of the originated opposite, circularity would necessarily occur, and that is impossible. The third division: that it is said: the substance remained only because the free agent preserves it; they said: this is also impossible because bringing into existence that which already exists and actualizing the actualized is impossible. The fourth division: that it remained only because there subsisted in the substance in the second moment accidents exigent for the permanence of that substance; then we say: that accident is either from the well-known accidents—which are colors, tastes, smells, wills, powers, and their likes—or it is an accident additional to them and different from them.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for baqa' and its subsequent refutation. The affirmers of baqa' argue that the substance, being possible of non-existence, needs a particularizer to keep it in existence, and that particularizer must be baqa'. This argument rests on three premises.

Premise 1: The substance is possible of permanence. Razi notes that this premise is solid but leads to the conclusion that accidents can also remain, which is problematic.

Premise 2: The substance is possible of non-existence in the second moment. Proof: its quiddity was receptive of non-existence before existence, so that receptivity persists.

Premise 3: Since the possible needs an exigent, that exigent must be either non-existential or existential, and either free or necessitating. This gives four divisions.

The first division (annihilation by not acting) is attributed to some early scholars but is rejected by those who say power cannot affect pure non-existence.

The second division (opposition by an accident called baqa') is the Mu'tazilite view. Razi then records the Ash'ari refutation with two proofs: (1) if opposition is mutual, why should the permanent cease rather than the new? In fact the permanent is stronger. (2) The occurrence of the new opposite presupposes the removal of the permanent, so if removal is caused by occurrence, circularity results.

The third division (preservation by a free agent) is dismissed as tantamount to re-creating what already exists, which is impossible.

The fourth division (through accidents) leaves open whether the accident is a usual one or a special accident called baqa', implying that either way, the need for a special accident is not established.

Key Terms
بقاء
جوهر
مخصص
فاعل مختار
مقتضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what does the first premise entail besides the possibility of the substance's permanence?
A) The substance's non-existence is impossible.
B) The permanence of accidents is valid.
C) The body is renewed state after state.
D) The particularizer must be baqa'.

Key Takeaway
The argument for baqa' rests on three premises: the substance can persist, can cease, and whatever is possible needs an exigent—which must be baqa'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment of your existence requires Allah's attribute of baqa' to sustain you, what changes in the way you think about your daily plans and your sense of security? Will you consciously rely on Him for your very next breath?
?
The substance could have been non-existent in the second moment — it is only Allah's particularization that keeps it. How does this affect your attachment to worldly things that you assume will 'just be there' tomorrow? Name one thing you need to release your grip on because it depends entirely on Allah's baqa'.
?
You are a 'possible' being in every moment. Does knowing that your persistence needs a fresh act of particularization from Allah make you more desperate in du'a? What will you pray for, starting today, to acknowledge that you have no independent staying power?

The Four Divisions of the Exigent and Refutation of the Second
Razi enumerates four divisions of the exigent and refutes the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four possible explanations for how a substance persists and the Ash'ari refutation of the second explanation.

Core Idea
Only the first and fourth divisions survive scrutiny; the second is refuted by two proofs, the third by impossibility.

Having established that the persistence of a substance requires an exigent, Razi lists four possible sources for that exigent: non-action, an opposite, preservation, or supporting accidents. The second explanation—that an opposite accident called baqa' causes persistence—was championed by the Mu'tazila but is refuted by the Ash'aris with two powerful arguments.

Guided Path
1
List the four divisions
Read the classification: the exigent can be non-existential or existential, and within each, free or necessitating, yielding four divisions.
Division 1: free agent does not annihilate; Division 2: an opposite accident; Division 3: free agent preserves; Division 4: accidents cause persistence.
2
Understand the refutation of Division 2
Study the two proofs against the Mu'tazilite view that baqa' is an opposite accident. The first proof argues that the permanent is stronger; the second shows circularity.
This is the core of the lesson.
3
Note the dismissal of Division 3 and the open end of Division 4
Division 3 is rejected as re-creation of what exists. Division 4 is left with a question: is the accident a common one or a special one?
This shows the debate is not fully resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وهو أحد قولي القاضي أبي بكر من أصحابنا وقول أبي الحسن الخياط من قدماء المعتزلة، وهو قول محمود الخوارزمي من متأخري المعتزلة - ومن الناس من أنكر ذلك، وقال: القدرة صفة مؤثرة، والعدم نفي محض، فإسناد العدم إلى القدرة محال. والقسم الثاني: أن يقال : إنه إنما بقي لأن له ضدّاً متى وجد لزم من وجوده عدمه، وإذا لم يوجد ذلك الضد لزم بقاؤه، وهذا مذهب أبي علي وأبي هاشم من المعتزلة وزعموا أن ذلك الضد هو عرض يخلقه الله تعالى لا في محل ويسمونه بالبقاء. والقاضي عبد الجبار بن أحمد وجمهور أصحابنا أبطلوا هذا القول من وجهين : الحجة الأولى : أن المضادة حاصلة من الجانبين فليس انقطاع الباقي لطريان الحادث أولى من اندفاع الحادث لوجود الباقي، وقالوا بل اندفاع الحادث لوجود الباقي أولى، وذلك لأن الحادث أضعف من الباقي بدليل أن الحادث لا يوجد إلا عند المقتضي، والباقي يبقى بدون المقتضي، وذلك يدل على أن الباقي أقوى. الحجة الثانية : أن طريان الضد الحادث مشروط بزوال الضد الباقي، فلو كان زوال الضد الباقي معللاً بطريان الضد الحادث لزم الدور، وهو محال. القسم الثالث: أن يقال : الجوهر إنما بقي لأن الفاعل المختار يبقيه، قالوا : وهذا أيضاً محال وذلك لأن إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل محال. والقسم الرابع : وهو أنه إنما بقي لأنه قام بالجوهر في الزمان الثاني أعراض مقتضية لبقاء ذلك الجوهر، ثم نقول : ذلك العرض إما أن يكون هو من الأعراض المشهورة - وهي الألوان والطعوم والروائح والإرادات والقدر وأمثالها - وإما أن يكون عرضاً زائداً عليها مغايراً لها.
And the affirmers of baqa' argued with this interpretation and said: The substance occurs in the second moment while it is possible for it to become non-existent, and the possible needs a particularizer, and it is impossible for that particularizer to be other than baqa', so the statement of the existence of baqa' is true. And this speech is based on premises: The first premise: that the substance occurs in the second moment—and the disagreement with al-Nazzam about it, as it is said, for he claimed that the body is renewed state after state—and the proof that the substance is possible of permanence in itself is that its quiddity being receptive of existence is a necessary concomitant of the essence, so this receptivity is always present; and if so, it is possible of existence in the second and third moment, and that necessitates that the body is possible of permanence. Know that this speech is solid except that it entails the validity of the permanence of accidents. The second premise: that the substance in the second moment is possible of non-existence; and some people claimed that after its occurrence non-existence becomes impossible for it. The proof of its being possible of non-existence is that its quiddity before its existence was described by non-existence, so it is established that this quiddity is receptive of non-existence, and this receptivity is a necessary concomitant of this quiddity, so this quiddity is always receptive of non-existence, and this necessitates that the body is possible of non-existence in the second moment. The third premise: since it is established that the body occurs in the second moment while it is possible to be non-existent, so the possible must have an exigent, and that exigent is either a non-existential matter or an existential matter, and on both suppositions it is either a free agent or a necessitating agent. So these are four divisions: The first division: that it is said: it perhaps remained because the free agent did not annihilate it, and this is the saying of those who said: annihilation by power is possible—and this is one of the two sayings of al-Qadi Abu Bakr from our companions, and the saying of Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat from the early Mu'tazila, and the saying of Mahmud al-Khwarizmi from the later Mu'tazila—and some people denied that and said: power is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation, so attributing non-existence to power is impossible. The second division: that it is said: it remained because it has an opposite; when it exists, its existence necessitates its non-existence, and if that opposite does not exist, its permanence is necessitated. This is the school of Abu 'Ali and Abu Hashim from the Mu'tazila, and they claimed that that opposite is an accident which Allah creates not in a locus and they call it baqa'. And al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad and the majority of our companions invalidated this statement from two aspects: The first proof: that opposition occurs from both sides, so the cessation of the permanent due to the occurrence of the originated is not more appropriate than the repulsion of the originated due to the existence of the permanent; rather they said the repulsion of the originated due to the existence of the permanent is more appropriate, because the originated is weaker than the permanent as evidenced by the fact that the originated does not exist except upon an exigent, while the permanent remains without an exigent, and that indicates that the permanent is stronger. The second proof: that the occurrence of the originated opposite is conditional upon the removal of the permanent opposite; so if the removal of the permanent opposite were caused by the occurrence of the originated opposite, circularity would necessarily occur, and that is impossible. The third division: that it is said: the substance remained only because the free agent preserves it; they said: this is also impossible because bringing into existence that which already exists and actualizing the actualized is impossible. The fourth division: that it remained only because there subsisted in the substance in the second moment accidents exigent for the permanence of that substance; then we say: that accident is either from the well-known accidents—which are colors, tastes, smells, wills, powers, and their likes—or it is an accident additional to them and different from them.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for baqa' and its subsequent refutation. The affirmers of baqa' argue that the substance, being possible of non-existence, needs a particularizer to keep it in existence, and that particularizer must be baqa'. This argument rests on three premises.

Premise 1: The substance is possible of permanence. Razi notes that this premise is solid but leads to the conclusion that accidents can also remain, which is problematic.

Premise 2: The substance is possible of non-existence in the second moment. Proof: its quiddity was receptive of non-existence before existence, so that receptivity persists.

Premise 3: Since the possible needs an exigent, that exigent must be either non-existential or existential, and either free or necessitating. This gives four divisions.

The first division (annihilation by not acting) is attributed to some early scholars but is rejected by those who say power cannot affect pure non-existence.

The second division (opposition by an accident called baqa') is the Mu'tazilite view. Razi then records the Ash'ari refutation with two proofs: (1) if opposition is mutual, why should the permanent cease rather than the new? In fact the permanent is stronger. (2) The occurrence of the new opposite presupposes the removal of the permanent, so if removal is caused by occurrence, circularity results.

The third division (preservation by a free agent) is dismissed as tantamount to re-creating what already exists, which is impossible.

The fourth division (through accidents) leaves open whether the accident is a usual one or a special accident called baqa', implying that either way, the need for a special accident is not established.

Key Terms
بقاء
جوهر
مخصص
ضد
فاعل مختار
مقتضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that baqa' is a necessary property of a substance, but the Mu'tazila argue it is a separate accident.
Correction: The passage shows that baqa' is posited as an accident created by Allah to prevent the substance from ceasing, not an intrinsic property.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof against the Mu'tazilite view of baqa' as an opposite accident?
A) The permanent is stronger than the originated.
B) The occurrence of the new opposite requires the removal of the permanent, leading to circularity if removal is caused by occurrence.
C) The opposite is not a real accident.
D) The free agent could annihilate instead.

Key Takeaway
Of the four explanations for persistence, only the first and fourth remain plausible; the second is refuted by two compelling arguments, and the third is dismissed as impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The third division—that God preserves a substance by directly keeping it in existence—was dismissed as impossible because it would mean recreating what already exists. Yet the Qur'an says, 'He creates you in the wombs of your mothers, creation after creation' (39:6). How do you reconcile the philosophical rejection of continuous recreation with the scriptural emphasis on God's ongoing creative act? Does this tension challenge you to deepen your understanding of how God sustains you every moment? What step can you take today to live in conscious dependence on His constant preservation?
?
The refutation of the second division shows that attributing persistence to a secondary cause (like an accident) leads to circularity. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes act as if your faith or good deeds persist because of your own efforts or habits—treating them as 'opposite accidents' that keep bad habits away? How does this passage expose the flaw in relying on anything other than God's direct will for your spiritual continuity? What would change in you if you truly believed that only God's free choice keeps your faith from collapsing?
?
Al-Razi leaves the fourth division open, allowing that persistence might be due to accidents like colors or a special accident called baqa'. This uncertainty means that the ultimate cause of persistence remains a matter of investigation. How does this intellectual humility affect your own attitude toward theological certainty? Are you willing to hold questions open before God, trusting Him even when philosophical arguments do not yield conclusive answers? What would it mean for you to live in that openness today, rather than pretending to have all the answers?

Razi's Rejection of Baqa' as a Separate Attribute
Razi presents his first proof against baqa' as a separate attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argues that Allah's eternal continuance (baqa') is not a separate attribute, based on the self-necessity of His essence.

Core Idea
Allah's self-necessity leaves no room for a separate attribute of continuance.

In this lesson, we examine Razi's argument against the Ash'arite majority view that baqa' is a real attribute. Razi anchors his proof in the foundational principle of wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi—necessary existence by itself. He will show that a separate attribute would compromise this self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the structure: statement of opposing view, then rejection, then proof.
2
Identify the key premise
The proof hinges on the premise that whatever is necessary by itself cannot be necessary by another. Find that premise in the text.
3
Apply the argument to baqa'
Understand how Razi uses this premise to reject baqa' as a separate attribute: if Allah remains by a separate attribute, then His continuance would be necessary by that attribute, contradicting self-necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني : في بقاء الباري سبحانه وتعالى أكثر أصحابنا قالوا : البقاء صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى تقتضي كونه باقيا ، وهذا عندنا باطل ويدل عليه وجهان: الحجة الأولى: أنا قد بينا أن ذاته تعالى واجبة الوجود لذاته من حيث هي هي، وواجب الوجود لذاته لا يكون واجب الوجود لغيره فاستحال أن يقال: إنه تعالى إنما بقي لبقاء قائم به.
The second chapter: On the baqa' (eternal continuance) of the Exalted and Supreme. Most of our companions said: Baqa' is an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah, Exalted, necessitating that He is eternal. This, according to us, is false, and it is indicated by two proofs: The first proof: We have clarified that His essence, Exalted, is necessary of existence by itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) as such, and that which is necessary of existence by itself cannot be necessary of existence by another. Therefore, it is impossible to say that He, Exalted, only remains due to a baqa' subsisting in Him.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage begins the second chapter on the divine attribute of baqa' (eternal continuance). The author states that most of his fellow Ash'arites hold that baqa' is a real attribute subsisting in Allah's essence that makes Him eternal. However, the author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) rejects this view and presents two proofs (only the first is given in this passage). The first proof is based on the self-necessity of Allah's existence. Since Allah's essence is necessary of existence by itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi), it cannot be necessary of existence by another. Therefore, attributing His eternal continuance to a separate attribute (baqa') would make Him dependent on that attribute for His continuance, contradicting His self-sufficiency. The argument is concise: if Allah's existence is necessary by Himself, then He cannot be necessary by another. Hence, baqa' cannot be a distinct cause of His eternal existence. The second proof is presumably given on the next page but is not included in this window.

Key Terms

واجب الوجود
صفة
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because human beings have baqa' as a separate accident, Allah must also have baqa' as a separate attribute.
Correction: Razi argues that Allah's existence is necessary by itself, so He does not require any separate attribute to remain; a separate attribute would imply dependence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is it impossible for Allah to have a separate attribute of baqa'?
A) Because baqa' is an accident and Allah has no accidents
B) Because Allah's essence is necessary of existence by itself, so it cannot be necessary by another
C) Because baqa' implies change
D) Because baqa' is a created attribute

Key Takeaway
Allah's eternal continuance is not due to a separate attribute but is a necessary consequence of His self-necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah is completely self-sufficient, or do you sometimes unconsciously act as if He needs your worship or obedience? How does this lesson challenge you to rely solely on Him?
?
If you hold that Allah's attributes are real and distinct, how do you reconcile that with His absolute oneness? Are you willing to accept that some attributes, like baqa', may not be separate entities but are inherent in His essence?
?
In what areas of your life do you still depend on created things for your security, even after affirming that Allah alone is necessary by Himself? What concrete step will you take this week to shift that dependence to Him alone?

First Proof: Permanence Cannot Be Through Another
Razi's first proof against permanence as an added attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, given that Allah is necessary of existence in Himself, His permanence cannot be through an added attribute.

Core Idea
The Necessary Existent cannot depend on anything else for its permanence.

Razi begins his refutation by establishing a fundamental principle: Allah is wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī (necessary of existence in Himself). From this, it follows logically that His permanence cannot be caused by an external attribute. This lesson unpacks that concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a statement of the opposing view, then a proof.
The proof flows directly from the definition of necessary existence.
2
Identify the key premise
Razi says Allah's essence is necessary of existence in itself (من حيث هي هي). What does this mean?
This premise is the foundation of the argument.
3
Apply the premise to permanence
If something is necessary in itself, it cannot derive its necessity—or its permanence—from something else. Why would saying He is permanent by a separate attribute violate this?
Think about what 'by a separate attribute' implies about dependence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes it is impossible to say Allah is permanent by an attribute subsisting in Him. How does this follow?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني : في بقاء الباري سبحانه وتعالى
أكثر أصحابنا قالوا : البقاء صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى تقتضي كونه باقيا ، وهذا عندنا باطل ويدل عليه وجهان:
الحجة الأولى: أنا قد بينا أن ذاته تعالى واجبة الوجود لذاته من حيث هي هي، وواجب الوجود لذاته لا يكون واجب الوجود لغيره فاستحال أن يقال: إنه تعالى إنما بقي لبقاء قائم به.
الحجة الثانية : أنه تعالى لو كان باقيًا ببقاء لكان ذلك البقاء باقيا ببقاء، فإما أن يكون ذلك البقاء باقيًا لذاته أو لغيره. فإن كان باقيًا لذاته والذات باقية لأجله فحينئذ يكون البقاء موجودًا باقيًا لذاته، وتكون الذات باقية تبعا لذلك البقاء، والمستقل أولى بأن يكون ذاتاً، والتابع أولى بأن يكون صفة، وحينئذ تصير الذات صفة والصفة ذاتا، وهو محال.
وأما إن قلنا : البقاء باق لأجل شيء غيره فذلك الغير إن كان الذات لزم الدور؛ لأن بقاء البقاء تبع لبقاء الذات، والذات تبع لبقاء البقاء، وإن كان متأخرًا كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول فيلزم التسلسل، و هو محال.
فهذا تمام الكلام في هذه المسألة
تلك ولمثبتي البقاء اختلافات في كيفية بقاء صفات الله تعالى، وشيء من الأقاويل لا يتوجه على هذه النكتة التي لخصناها، وبالله التوفيق.
The second chapter: On the permanence of the Creator, the Exalted.
Most of our companions said: Permanence is an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah, the Exalted, necessitating His being permanent. This, according to us, is false, and is indicated by two arguments:
The first proof: We have already explained that His essence, the Exalted, is necessary of existence in itself from whatever aspect it is, and that which is necessary of existence in itself cannot be necessary of existence through another, so it is impossible to say that the Exalted is permanent only due to a permanence subsisting in Him.
The second proof: If the Exalted were permanent by a permanence, then that permanence would itself be permanent by a permanence. Then either that permanence is permanent in itself or through another. If it is permanent in itself, and the essence is permanent because of it, then the permanence would be an existent permanent in itself, and the essence would be permanent as a consequence of that permanence. The independent is more deserving of being an essence, and the dependent is more deserving of being an attribute. Then the essence would become an attribute and the attribute would become an essence, which is impossible.
If we say that the permanence is permanent for the sake of something else, then if that other is the essence, circularity follows because the permanence of the permanence is dependent on the permanence of the essence, and the essence is dependent on the permanence of the permanence. If it is something posterior, the discussion applies to it as in the first case, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible.
This is the complete discussion on this issue.
Those who affirm permanence have disagreements about the modality of the permanence of Allah's attributes, and none of their statements can withstand the point we have summarized. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ash'ari view that Allah's attribute of permanence (baqa') is a superadded attribute subsisting in His essence. Razi presents two proofs. The first relies on the principle that Allah is wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī (necessary of existence in Himself). Therefore, His permanence cannot be through something other than Himself; to say He is permanent by a separate permanence would make Him dependent on something else for His permanence, contradicting His self-sufficiency. The second proof examines the implications of affirming an attribute of permanence. If Allah is permanent by a permanence, then that permanence itself would need to be permanent. The permanence of the permanence can either be by itself or by something else. If by itself, then the permanence would be an independent existent (like a substance), and the essence would be dependent on it, reversing the ontological order: the essence (which should be independent) becomes a dependent attribute, and the attribute (which should be dependent) becomes an independent essence. This is impossible. If the permanence is permanent by something else (e.g., the essence), then circularity results: the permanence depends on the essence for its permanence, and the essence depends on the permanence for its permanence. If that other is something else (a third entity), we have an infinite regress. Both are impossible. Thus, the idea of an additional attribute of permanence for Allah is untenable. Razi concludes that the discussion is complete and that other opinions cannot refute this point.

Key Terms
بقاء
واجب الوجود
صفة
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah's permanence can be a superadded attribute just like creatures have a quality of persistence.
Correction: Creatures are contingent, so their persistence is through an accident. But Allah is necessary of existence in Himself; therefore, His permanence must be identical with His essence, not an addition.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of Razi's first proof?
A) Permanence is a necessary attribute of Allah.
B) Allah is necessary of existence in Himself.
C) Attributes cannot subsist in Allah.
D) Permanence requires a cause.

Key Takeaway
Allah's permanence is intrinsic to His essence because He is necessary of existence in Himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself relying on created things—like people, possessions, or status—to give you a sense of stability or permanence? How does knowing that even the Creator's permanence is not through anything else challenge your own dependencies? Commit to one thing you will stop depending on today.
?
If Allah's existence and attributes are self-sufficient, then your relationship with Him is based on His perfect independence. Have you ever treated your 'iman as if it depends on circumstances or other people? This week, what step will you take to root your faith directly in Allah, not in external supports?
?
Razi argues that adding an attribute to Allah implies need. In your own life, do you cling to extra 'attributes' or roles about yourself to feel worthwhile? What would it mean for you to find your worth purely in Allah's sufficiency, not in what others give you?

Second Proof: Absurd Consequences of an Attribute of Permanence
Razi's second proof: showing absurd consequences of an added permanence attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three impossible consequences (inversion, circularity, infinite regress) that follow from affirming an added attribute of permanence for Allah.

Core Idea
An attribute of permanence leads to an endless chain of absurdities.

The second proof is more elaborate: if Allah were permanent by a separate attribute, that attribute itself would need to be permanent. Razi then examines the possibilities for how that attribute could be permanent, showing that every option leads to an impossibility—either reversing the roles of essence and attribute, or falling into circularity or infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the full second proof
Read the Arabic and translation for the second proof. Note that it branches into two main cases.
2
Identify the two branches
If permanence is permanent, it must be either permanent in itself or through another. Find where Razi discusses each branch.
These branches correspond to two different absurdities.
3
Analyze the first branch
If permanence is permanent in itself, it would be an independent existent, and the essence would be dependent on it. What is wrong with this?
Think about which should be substance and which attribute.
4
Analyze the second branch
If permanence is permanent through another, that other could be the essence (circularity) or something else (infinite regress). Understand each.
Circularity: A depends on B, B depends on A. Regress: an endless chain.
5
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that all these are impossible, so the original claim of an added permanence is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني : في بقاء الباري سبحانه وتعالى
أكثر أصحابنا قالوا : البقاء صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى تقتضي كونه باقيا ، وهذا عندنا باطل ويدل عليه وجهان:
الحجة الأولى: أنا قد بينا أن ذاته تعالى واجبة الوجود لذاته من حيث هي هي، وواجب الوجود لذاته لا يكون واجب الوجود لغيره فاستحال أن يقال: إنه تعالى إنما بقي لبقاء قائم به.
الحجة الثانية : أنه تعالى لو كان باقيًا ببقاء لكان ذلك البقاء باقيا ببقاء، فإما أن يكون ذلك البقاء باقيًا لذاته أو لغيره. فإن كان باقيًا لذاته والذات باقية لأجله فحينئذ يكون البقاء موجودًا باقيًا لذاته، وتكون الذات باقية تبعا لذلك البقاء، والمستقل أولى بأن يكون ذاتاً، والتابع أولى بأن يكون صفة، وحينئذ تصير الذات صفة والصفة ذاتا، وهو محال.
وأما إن قلنا : البقاء باق لأجل شيء غيره فذلك الغير إن كان الذات لزم الدور؛ لأن بقاء البقاء تبع لبقاء الذات، والذات تبع لبقاء البقاء، وإن كان متأخرًا كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول فيلزم التسلسل، و هو محال.
فهذا تمام الكلام في هذه المسألة
تلك ولمثبتي البقاء اختلافات في كيفية بقاء صفات الله تعالى، وشيء من الأقاويل لا يتوجه على هذه النكتة التي لخصناها، وبالله التوفيق.
The second chapter: On the permanence of the Creator, the Exalted.
Most of our companions said: Permanence is an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah, the Exalted, necessitating His being permanent. This, according to us, is false, and is indicated by two arguments:
The first proof: We have already explained that His essence, the Exalted, is necessary of existence in itself from whatever aspect it is, and that which is necessary of existence in itself cannot be necessary of existence through another, so it is impossible to say that the Exalted is permanent only due to a permanence subsisting in Him.
The second proof: If the Exalted were permanent by a permanence, then that permanence would itself be permanent by a permanence. Then either that permanence is permanent in itself or through another. If it is permanent in itself, and the essence is permanent because of it, then the permanence would be an existent permanent in itself, and the essence would be permanent as a consequence of that permanence. The independent is more deserving of being an essence, and the dependent is more deserving of being an attribute. Then the essence would become an attribute and the attribute would become an essence, which is impossible.
If we say that the permanence is permanent for the sake of something else, then if that other is the essence, circularity follows because the permanence of the permanence is dependent on the permanence of the essence, and the essence is dependent on the permanence of the permanence. If it is something posterior, the discussion applies to it as in the first case, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible.
This is the complete discussion on this issue.
Those who affirm permanence have disagreements about the modality of the permanence of Allah's attributes, and none of their statements can withstand the point we have summarized. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ash'ari view that Allah's attribute of permanence (baqa') is a superadded attribute subsisting in His essence. Razi presents two proofs. The first relies on the principle that Allah is wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī (necessary of existence in Himself). Therefore, His permanence cannot be through something other than Himself; to say He is permanent by a separate permanence would make Him dependent on something else for His permanence, contradicting His self-sufficiency. The second proof examines the implications of affirming an attribute of permanence. If Allah is permanent by a permanence, then that permanence itself would need to be permanent. The permanence of the permanence can either be by itself or by something else. If by itself, then the permanence would be an independent existent (like a substance), and the essence would be dependent on it, reversing the ontological order: the essence (which should be independent) becomes a dependent attribute, and the attribute (which should be dependent) becomes an independent essence. This is impossible. If the permanence is permanent by something else (e.g., the essence), then circularity results: the permanence depends on the essence for its permanence, and the essence depends on the permanence for its permanence. If that other is something else (a third entity), we have an infinite regress. Both are impossible. Thus, the idea of an additional attribute of permanence for Allah is untenable. Razi concludes that the discussion is complete and that other opinions cannot refute this point.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adding an attribute of permanence to Allah is a harmless theological position.
Correction: Razi shows it necessarily leads to one of three logical absurdities: inversion of essence and attribute, circular reasoning, or infinite regress. Hence it is not harmless but rationally untenable.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof, what happens if the attribute of permanence is permanent in itself?
A) It leads to circularity.
B) It leads to infinite regress.
C) It causes the essence to become an attribute and the attribute to become an essence.
D) It proves that permanence is not needed.

Key Takeaway
Affirming an added attribute of permanence for Allah leads to inversion of essence and attribute, circularity, or infinite regress—all impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's argument shows that even a seemingly simple attribute like 'permanence' leads to contradictions if treated as separate from Allah. Are there any attributes or qualities you habitually ascribe to Allah without thinking through their logical implications? How will you ensure your understanding of Allah remains free from such subtle errors?
?
This lesson forces you to choose: either Allah is permanent by His very essence, or you fall into absurdity. Have you ever consciously affirmed that Allah’s permanence is not added to His essence? If not, what would it cost you to accept this conclusion wholeheartedly?
?
Consider the humility required to abandon a popular or comfortable belief when reason exposes its impossibility. Is there any belief about Allah you hold that you would be unwilling to test with this kind of rigorous reasoning? Why?

Second Proof Against the Ash'ari View on Baqa'
Razi presents a conditional proof against the Ash'ari view of baqa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second argument against the Ash'ari doctrine of an eternal attribute of baqa', which exposes circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
An eternal attribute leads to circularity or infinite regress, undermining its necessity.

Imam Razi now presents a second, sharper argument against the Ash'ari claim that God's eternity is due to an eternal attribute called baqa'. He shows that if baqa' itself needs to be eternal, the entire scheme collapses into either reversing the roles of essence and attribute or generating an infinite chain. This short but powerful proof is a hallmark of Razi's logical precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument structure
Follow the conditional 'if... then...' and identify the two branches Razi sets up: either the attribute is eternal by itself or by something else.
The argument is a proof by contradiction assuming the Ash'ari position.
2
Analyze the first branch
If baqa' is eternal by itself, it becomes independent, and the essence becomes dependent. This reverses their roles, which is impossible.
The reversal contradicts the definitions of essence (self-subsistent) and attribute (dependent).
3
Analyze the second branch
If baqa' is eternal by something else, that something else leads either to circularity (if simultaneous) or infinite regress (if posterior). Both are impossible.
Thus the initial assumption that God is eternal by an attribute is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أنه تعالى لو كان باقيًا ببقاء لكان ذلك البقاء باقيا ببقاء، فإما أن يكون ذلك البقاء باقيًا لذاته أو لغيره. فإن كان باقيًا لذاته والذات باقية لأجله فحينئذ يكون البقاء موجودًا باقيًا لذاته، وتكون الذات باقية تبعا لذلك البقاء، والمستقل أولى بأن يكون ذاتاً، والتابع أولى بأن يكون صفة، وحينئذ تثير الذات صفة والصفة ذاتا، وهو محال. هو الذات لزم [؟] وأما إن قلنا : البقاء باق لأجل شيء غيره فذلك الغير إن كان الدور؛ لأن بقاء البقاء تبع لبقاء الذات، والذات تبع لبقاء البقاء، وإن كان متأخرًا كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول فيلزم التسلسل، و هو محال. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذه المسألة. ولمثبتي البقاء اختلافات في كيفية بقاء صفات الله تعالى، وشيء من الأقاويل لا يتوجه على هذه النكتة التي لخصناها، وبالله التوفيق.
The second proof: If the Exalted were eternal by an eternity, then that eternity would be eternal by an eternity. Then either that eternity is eternal by itself or by something else. If it is eternal by itself and the essence is eternal for its sake, then the eternity would exist, eternal by itself, and the essence would be eternal as a consequence of that eternity, and the independent is more deserving of being an essence, and the dependent is more deserving of being an attribute. Then the essence would become an attribute and the attribute an essence, which is impossible. [Unclear: is the essence necessitated?] And if we say: the eternity is eternal for the sake of something else, then that something else, if it is simultaneous, leads to circularity because the eternity of the eternity is dependent on the eternity of the essence, and the essence is dependent on the eternity of the eternity; and if it is posterior, the discussion applies to it as to the first, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. This completes the discussion of this issue. Those who affirm eternity have differences regarding how the attributes of God are eternal, but none of their statements avoid this point we have summarized. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a proof. Razi presents the second argument against the Ash'ari position that God is eternal by an eternal attribute (baqa'). The argument is structured as a conditional: if God were eternal by an attribute, then that attribute would itself need to be eternal, leading to a regress. He splits into two possibilities: either the attribute is eternal by itself or by something else. If by itself, then the attribute becomes independent and the essence becomes dependent, reversing their roles, which is absurd. If by something else, that something else leads either to circularity or infinite regress. Thus the assumption is invalid. He concludes that no version of the attribute theory escapes this criticism.

بقاء
ذات
صفة
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument itself is circular.
Correction: The argument exposes circularity in the opponent's view, not in its own reasoning.

Checkpoint
What does the second argument against the Ash'ari view on baqa' entail?
A) It proves that God is eternal by an attribute.
B) It shows that positing an eternal attribute leads to circularity or infinite regress.
C) It establishes the impossibility of God's existence.
D) It affirms the Ash'ari position.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof shows that the Ash'ari doctrine of baqa' leads to either role reversal or circularity/regress, making it rationally untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's argument exposes that assuming an attribute of eternity forces a choice between role reversal and infinite regress. Have you ever held a belief about God that, upon honest reflection, led to a similar logical contradiction? Are you willing to abandon that belief for the sake of consistency?
?
This proof requires you to think deeply about what it means for God to be eternal. Does your understanding of God's eternity rest more on memorized formulas or on rational conviction? Would you be able to defend it if someone pressed you with such an argument?
?
The Ash'ari position on baqa' might be familiar to you from your studies. After seeing Razi's criticism, do you feel a tension between following a school and following reason? What would it cost you to submit your theological commitments to rational scrutiny even when they clash with tradition?
